Preface
This thesis is the ﬁnal assignment of the MSc programme in the ﬁeld of Civil Engi-
neering. The work has been carried out by Isabella Aspøy at the Technical University
of Denmark in collaboration with Rambøll.
The thesis deals with analysing of structural elements in ultimate- and serviceability
limit state by numerical modelling providing a optimized lower bound solution and
by minimizing the complementary elastic energy, respectively. The results will be
compared with experimental tests from the literature.
The work has been supervised by Professor Linh C. Hoang, Bent Feddersen and
Kasper Paaske Larsen both from Rambøll, all of which have contributed with valu-
able knowledges, encouragement and advisement. Furthermore, I like to thank for
professional discussions and for always being at disposal. Lately, thanks to Rambøll
(Jesper Miller Andersen) for providing diﬀerent tools and making oﬃce space avail-
able throughout the process and to Julie Lund Zaﬁri, Daniel Vejsgaard Laursen and
Eirik Aspøy for help and support.
Lyngby, February 1st, 2017
Isabella Aspøy,
Student number.: 113793

==PAGE:7==
Summary
Rambøll has developed a numerical tool to determine the ultimate load carrying
capacity of reinforced concrete structures based on a rigid-perfectly plastic material
model.
In this project, typical structural elements and loading conﬁgurations encountered
in practice is modelled by use of the numerical tool in order to study their behaviour
at the ultimate limit state. The solution providing the behaviour in the ultimate
limit state is determined by an optimized lower bound solution limit by the yield
surface implemented in the program. The same element is then analysed in respect
to the serviceability limit state i.e the elastic cracked behaviour. This has been done
by a numerical procedure, which search for the equilibrium solution that minimizes
the complementary elastic energy of the system. The results is then compared with
experimental tests from the literature.
The test-series use to compare with the result from the numerical tool constitutes,
simple supported T-beams loaded by two symmetrical point loads with diﬀerent re-
inforcement degrees, deep beams with diﬀerent cross-section loaded by a uniform
distributed load at the top face, deep beams with diﬀerent reinforcement degree
loaded by a uniform distributed load at the bottom face and continuous beams with
holes.
A basic understanding of the program is outlined and simple tests of the yields sur-
face implemented in the program is done for diﬀerent basic cases. In the process
of modelling the T-beams the importance of the model design in order to produce
usable results was discovered. From the comparison with the experimental tests
of T-beams a good compliance between load-carrying capacity provided by the nu-
merical tool and the test-results was found. This is also the case for the behaviour
in respect to the failure mode. In the serviceability limit state results provided by
the numerical tools are compared with stirrups stresses measured during the experi-
ment. The results where found to be parallel but with a horizontal translation. The
horizontal translation deceased as the reinforcement degree increases. The angle
of the principal direction is compared with the the angle of the cracks determined
by studying pictures taken during the experiment. A good agreement was found
between the two angles. Further more redistribution of stresses is determined by
considering the angel of the principle direction in the elastic range and the ultimate
limit state. Even though the stresses has to be rotated more at lower reinforcement
degree the numerical tool is able to predict the structures ability to do so.
v
==PAGE:8==
For some of the deep beams the numerical tool is found to be limited by failure
at the support estimating 25% lower load-carrying capacity than found by the ex-
periment. For the beams not governed by failure at the support a good agreement
is found between the result provided by the optimized lower bound solution and
the test result. Furthermore it is discovered that the direction of principal stresses
agrees with the crack pattern found in the experiments, providing information that
the stress distribution found by the numerical tools gives a rather good estimation
of the structural elements behaviour.
For statically indeterminate structures, the numerical tool is found to overestimate
the structures ability to redistribute the stresses. Hence creating a to optimized
solution. However, if the structure is forced to create the same load to distribution
as found for the reference beam, the results are found to agree with the test results
with or without holes.
vi

Chapter 1
Introduction
Dealing with design of concrete structures, the ultimate load capacity is often of in-
terest and have got a lot of attention in the ﬁeld of research. However, even though
the theory of plasticity may be traced back to Galileo [1] it is only in the last decades
the theory of plastic has been adopted in the ﬁeld of research in respect to reinforced
concrete elements using the plastic properties of the concrete and reinforcement. Us-
ing the theory in determination of the load-carrying capacity is called limit analysis.
The theory of plasticity assumes that materials can deform plastically at a certain
load level under constant load, hence achieve full utilization of both concrete and
reinforcement. The development of the plastic theory has made it possible to treat
varies of problems from an analytical point of view.
The solutions either depend on a lower bound solution or a upper bound solution
which requires a rather good intuitively understanding of the structure. Therefore,
choosing the wrong (not optimal) lower bound solution will produce more reinforce-
ment than necessary and thereby generate more cost than necessary. For instance
in design of concrete walls in a entire building. However, using the upper bound
solution and miss a critical collapse mechanism can have fatal consequences.
However, the ultimate state of reinforced structures have been the subject of com-
prehensive research which provides a rather large catalogue of known solution and
reﬁned theories.
As the solutions become more an more reﬁned approaching the exact solution in the
ultimate state, the structures become slender an less reinforcement is needed. This
gives rise to new problems in respect to the serviceability limit state.
The low reinforcement degrees reduces the ductility of the structure. However, in
order to go from the elastic range to the ultimate state the stresses has to be re-
distributed. If the ductility of the structure is insuﬃcient, large cracks will occur
due to early yield of the reinforcement. Thus, the structure is not ductile enough
to rotate the concrete stresses from the cracked elastic state into the ultimate limit
state.
Furthermore it may even lead to failure before the ultimate limit state is reached
due to unpredicted distribution of the stresses. In order to assume full utilization
of the reinforcement, in compliance with the theory of plasticity, it requires that all
the rebars yields before failure occur. However if some of the rebars is not activated
by cracking before yielding of the ﬁrst bar, it may lead to early failure. Knowledge,
1
==PAGE:16==
1.1.
Motivation
Introduction
however of how the stresses is redistributed in the structure until the ultimate limit
state is rather limited.
In the resent years several programs has been developed using ﬁnite element analysis
taking in to account non-linear incremental in order to predict the behaviour of
reinforced concrete structures from when load is ﬁrst applied until failure. However
this is rather complicated due to varies factors and also time-consuming.
1.1
Motivation
As an alternative to the time-consuming non-linear incremental FE-analysis of rein-
forced concrete (RC) structures, Rambøll has developed a numerical tool to deter-
mine the ultimate load carrying capacity of RC structures based on a rigid-perfectly
plastic material model providing a optimized lower bound solution. The program
was original developed to optimize the amount of reinforcement in stabilizing walls
by providing a optimal lower bound solution. The program is named Nucleus. The
overall objective of this thesis is to determine rather the failure envelope implemented
in the program complies with the reality and not just the theory of practicality.
This kind of analysis (optimized lower bound solution ) assumes the structure to
have suﬃcient ductility. Thus, for a safe use of this type of analysis, it is necessary
to ensure enough ductility or to limit the extent of stress redistribution. The extent
of ductility required depends on the degree of stress redistribution when the struc-
ture moves from the cracked elastic state into the ultimate limit state. To assess
the degree of stress redistribution, it is necessary to at least have an estimate of the
stress ﬁeld in the elastic cracked state. The program has therefore been extended
to include the serviceability limit state. Thus, acknowledging the problem the opti-
mized solution gives rise to.
In this project, typical structural elements and loading conﬁgurations encountered
in practice will be modelled by use of the numerical tool in order to study their
behaviour at the ultimate limit state. The same elements will then be analysed
assuming elastic cracked behaviour. This will be done by a numerical procedure,
which search for the equilibrium solution that minimizes the complementary elastic
energy of the system. The results will be compared with experimental tests from
the literature.
It is expected that the comparison with test results as well as the diﬀerence between
the stress distributions in the elastic and in the plastic state, can be used to ex-
tract important information regarding ductility requirements as well as the eﬀective
compressive strength of concrete.
2
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:17==
Chapter 2
Reinforced Concrete
When designing structural elements it must be insured that members have suﬃcient
capacity to withstand the forces action on the structure. However it is also impor-
tant to obtain knowledge about the stress distribution in the structure in order to
control cracks.
In this chapter the behaviour of concrete in tension will be outlined. Then an attempt
to describe the caking behaviour of concrete will follow. Lastly an introduction of
some parameters established from previous research in order to account for strength
reduction due to cracks will be provided.
2.1
Cracked Reinforced Concrete
Dealing with concrete structures cracking is an inevitable factor, regardless of whether
a crack is considered in respect to the environment causing the reinforcement to cor-
rode, leak of waterproof structures causing all kinds of damages or in respect the
strength reduction and behaviour of reinforced concrete members which is highly
aﬀected by the developments of cracks [2]. It is therefore essential to obtain knowl-
edge about the developing of cracks and thereby be able to predict if or when they
will occur. The knowledge is especially useful in the design phase of structural mem-
bers, giving the possibility to include crack formation and crack control to obtain a
satisfying behaviour specially in the serviceability limit state.
For elastic homogeneous materials, the stiﬀness is a well diﬃdent parameter simply
expressed by the constitutive relation by Hook’s law σ = εE. However dealing with
reinforced structures the deﬁnition of the stiﬀness is not so simple. For instance,
if load is applied still keeping the specimen in the linear elastic range and then
reloaded, the response may be signiﬁcant diﬀerent the second time due to changed
stiﬀness of the concrete caused by cracks. These cracks, called initial cracks, occur
when the tensile strength of the concrete is reached. Thus in the attempt to un-
derstand the behaviour of cracked reinforcement it is important to understand the
behaviour of concrete in tension.
From research is has been discovered that the stress-strain relation for tension in
the concrete have the same nature as the stress-strain relation for compression [3].
The important diﬀerence is the deceasing parts of the curve which is governed by
the processing of cracking. A typical stress-deformation curve for normal strength
concrete specimen is illustrated in Figure 2.1.
3
==PAGE:18==
2.1.
Cracked Reinforced Concrete
Reinforced Concrete
Figure 2.1. Typical stress-deformation curve of a uniaxial tension test of concrete.
In order to understand the development of tension stresses in the concrete the curve
is separated in to the following phases referring to the letters on the curve [4]:
A-B: The concrete behave linear elastic with the stiﬀness Ec.
B-C: Microcracking (also called internal cracks) causing a non-linear behaviour of
the concrete.
C: The ﬁrst principle stress reaches the concrete tension strength fc and the ﬁrst
initial cracks occur.
C-D: As cracking progresses stresses are still transmitted in the crack until a certain
crack width (point D). This is called concrete softening.
In phase C-D the concrete changes properties and can no longer be assumed as
a elastic material which is the reason for most of the diﬃculties in predicting the
behaviour. In order to attempt to incorporate this behaviour into the theory of
reinforced concrete members the relation may be described by two relations; One
depended of stresses as a function of strain and the other as a function of the crack
opening, illustrated in 2.2 and 2.3 respectively.
4
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:19==
Reinforced Concrete
2.1. Cracked Reinforced Concrete
Figure 2.2. Elastic stress-strain relation disre-
garding the non-linearity.
Figure 2.3. Concrete softening.
Where the non-linearities is disregarded in order to simplify matters, the behaviour
is considered linear elastic with Ec until ft. The simpliﬁcation is assumed admis-
sible, thus the non-linearities occurs only for relative small deformation in respect
to the total deformation umax. In PhD Thesis [2] a model has been provided to
include concrete softening in the calculation by assuming a ﬁctitious Young’s mod-
ulus. However, in this report the concept of concrete softening will only be used to
understand the behaviour of the test-specimen and redistribution of stresses in the
comparison.
The tensile strength can either be determined from standard tensile test of the con-
crete or by the concrete compression strength ft = √0.1fc, where fc is the cylinder
compressive strength [1]. In general the tensile strength for structural elements is
lower than the tensile strength found by standard test specimens, as Figure 2.1.
Hence, a eﬀective strength ftef is introduced.
ftef = νft
(2.1)
where ν is the eﬀectiveness factor. The reduction of the strength due to additional
microcracks, eﬀecting the stiﬀness of the concrete. Microcracks may be caused before
applied load by the ribs on the reinforcement, shrinkages stresses, transportation etc.
Load-induced microcrack may also occur. However, the eﬀective tensile strength is
a diﬃcult parameter to deﬁne because it depends a lot on the circumstances. These
will be presented in Section 2.2.
With this knowledge of how concrete behave in tension, the development of cracks
in the elastic range of a reinforced concrete member will now be explained:
When a reinforced concrete structure is loaded for the ﬁrst time, the stresses increases
in the structure until the ﬁrst principle stresses σ1 reaches the eﬀective concrete ten-
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
5
==PAGE:20==
2.1.
Cracked Reinforced Concrete
Reinforced Concrete
sile strength feft. This phase is called the untracked state. When the ﬁrst principle
stress excised feft cracking initiates. These cracks are called as the initial cracks
formed perpendicular to σ1 and the reinforcement bars get into actions. The initial
cracks is the ﬁrst visible cracks. Even though the concrete is cracked, stresses will
still be transmitted in the crack until a certain crack width denoted as the critical
crack width wcr, see Figure 2.3. This phenomenon as mentioned is called concrete
softening. If the crack is greater than wcr the crack is assumed stress free. The cross-
section is now assumed fully cracked and the ﬁrst principal stresses in the concrete
becomes zero and the uniaxial concrete compression stress rotates to a new direction.
The angle of the cracks in the fully cracked phase is deﬁned as θe and is parallel to
the second principle direction. Hereafter the concrete is assumed as a material with
zero tensile strength an the behaviour will be governed by the strain-stress relation
of the reinforcement.
In the process of going from the elastic range to the plastic range, uniaxial con-
crete compression stress has to rotate again into a new direction rotation from θe
to θp. θp deﬁning the principal direction at failure forming failure crack parallel to θp.
Hence, in principle three diﬀerent crack phases occur: Initial cracks, fully cracked
phase and cracks at failure, see Figure 2.4.
Figure 2.4. Orthotropic panel in pure shear [1].
where the initial cracks, for the case of pure shear, will be formed under 45◦inde-
pendent of the reinforcement as indicated on Figure 2.4. The cracks will be turned
in the direction with the largest reinforcement ratio.
2.1.1
Tensile stresses in rebars
When a crack is formed crossing a rebar the maximum stresses in the repar will occur
at the crack creating an uneven stress distribution in the bar. These cracks, going
all the way through to the rebar, are called primary cracks. Due to debonding cause
by the ribs of the bars, the maximum stresses will occur in a distance corresponding
to lo called the slip length. However, in the rest of the bar the stresses is uniformly
distribution and smaller than in the primary crack as illustrated in Figure 2.5.
6
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark

Reinforced Concrete
2.2. Eﬀectiveness factor
Figure 2.5. Schematic stress distribution in a rebar [5].
2.2
Eﬀectiveness factor
The strength of concrete found by standard specimens is often found to be larger
than the strength observed by tests of structural elements, as also mentioned in
respect to the concrete tensile strength. The main reason is strength reduction due
to microcracks and cracking. In order to account for the strength reduction and the
limited ductility of concrete a eﬀective strength is introduced. The eﬀective strength
is found by multiplying the concrete strength with an eﬀectiveness factor νfc. The
following eﬀectiveness factor is introduced on the tensile and compression strength
of the concrete depending on the stress state:
Eﬀectiveness for shear when comparison with test is set to [6][7][8]
νv = 0.8 −
fc
200MPa
(2.2)

factor is set to
νv = 0.7 −
fc
200MPa
(2.3)
Eﬀectiveness factor in relation pure normal stresses is denotes as νv and is set equal
to νm if the normal stresses i caused by bending is set to [9]
νm = 0.98 −
fc

(2.4)
νv is set to 1 if the normal stresses is burly caused by a pure normal force.
For the concrete tensile strength ν is varied between 0.5-0.85 for pure shear and
stresses cause by ﬂexural, respectively. In few cases like triaxial stress ﬁeld preventing
cracks ν = 1. In this report ν = 0.5 will only be used

==PAGE:22==
==PAGE:23==
Chapter 3
Nucleus
In this Chapter the basic principle of the program will be outlined, followed by an ex-
planation of how a model is created in the program and how the results are presented.
Nucleus is a proprietary system for integrated engineering analysis and design. The
program is able to calculated the Ultimate Limit state (ULS) and the Serviceabil-
ity Limit state (SLS) for reinforced concrete members. The solution is provided as
utilization of the member or as an optimization problem. In this thesis only the
utilization function of the program is used.
Nucleus is based on a framework called Newton which contains all the information
regarding the elements, nodes, material model, the solver etc. Newton reformulates
the problem into a convex optimization problem. A convex optimization problem is
a special class of mathematical optimization problem use to solve various of prob-
lems across disciplines. Newton uses a third part solver called moske [10]
The program is based on two solvers, one that relies on the lower bound theo-
rem(ULS) and one on the energy method (SLS), optimizing the complimentary
elastic energy in each element. The overall approach for the energy method will
be elaborated in Chapter 5.
In order to assemble the optimization problem, all
solvers that usages this approach, uses the same set of methods, with minor changes
in order to ﬁt each speciﬁc problem. The theoretical background for the program
will not be outlined in this report, for this the reader is refereed to Newton Theory
Manual [11]. In this report the overall approach will be outlined.
The lower bound solution (ULS) requires, that the chosen stress ﬁeld fulﬁls the
equilibrium at all points [12]. The lower bound therm also states that the chosen
stress ﬁeld must be safe. In order to be that, the solution must not violate the yield
criterion at any point within the structure.
Nucleus is manly developed in order to analyse stabilizing reinforced concrete walls
primarily subjected to in-plane forces (nx, ny, nxy). In order to provide a solution
Nucleus employs the dual conic failure envelope for reinforced concrete disk (compos-
ite material) developed using rigid-plastic theory and the failure criteria for concrete
[1].
9
==PAGE:24==

Failure envelope
Nucleus
3.1
Failure envelope
In this section a disk with low reinforcement in two directions will ﬁrst be examined,
in order to establish some principle terms an assumptions for the yield condition,
followed by a presentation of the condition for an orthotropic reinforced disk should
satisfy, in order to carry a prescribed stress ﬁeld
The concrete is considered as a rigid-plastic material obeying the modiﬁed Coulomb
criterion with zero tensile cut-oﬀ, thus the concrete is considered to have zero tensile
strength in Nucleus as illustrated in Figure 3.1. In respect to the reinforcement, the
strain-stress relation presented in Figure 3.2 i assumed for tension and compression
equal the yield strength fy. However, in Nucleus is the compression capacity of rein-
forcement set to zero corresponding to the blue line in Figure 3.2. The reinforcement
bars are only considered to be able to carry longitudinal tensile strength. This is
also assumed for the concrete providing a safe load-carrying capacity.

the stresses of the concrete as σcx, σcy and τcxy. The stresses in the reinforcement will
be considered evenly distributed over the thickness of the disk, called the equivalent
stresses of the bars. The sum of the concrete stresses and the textitequivalent stresses
of the bars is called the total stress. Introducing the reinforcement degree for the

Modelling
The geometrical deign of the model is ﬁrst modelled in FEM-Design from Strusoft
and then exported to Nucleus. The information exported to Nucleus from FEM-
design is the geometry, load cases, load combinations, location of supports and
stringers if this is relevant.
Thus, all the reinforcement can be modelled in the
Nucleus user interface, see Figure 3.5.

==PAGE:28==
3.1.
Failure envelope
Nucleus
Figure 3.5. Example of model exported from FEM-design.
The support conditions are deﬁned in Nucleus. The material properties is also ex-
ported from FEM-design to Nucleus but can easily be changed in the user interface
where the relevant ν −factor also can be deﬁned. It is only necessary to specify if
the material should be rigid in FEM-design, before exporting the model to Nucleus,
because this can not be added in Nucleus. If this is the case, a new martial should by
added in FEM-design by the name "Rigid". When the material is exported, Nucleus
will automatic set the material properties for elements with this material to rigid.
However, using rigid material excludes the SLS calculation, thus the marital has not
yet been included in the calculations.
Nucleus provides to diﬀerent types of mesh using the mesh generator in Robot Struc-
tural Analysis (autodisk): Coons, Figure 3.6 and Delaunay, Figure 3.7. Delaunay
are favourable when dealing with arbitrary geometry, due to the fact that it creates
more elements at corners distributing the stresses.
Figure 3.6. Coones Mesh
Figure 3.7. Delaunay Mesh
Nucleus provides the possibility to create mesh reinforcement with or without close
stirrups at all free edges, stringers and U-bars. If openings are found in the structure
as often seen in wall, and reinforcement mesh with stirrups are used as reinforcement,
Nucleus automatically assumes that the mesh also is closed along the edge of the hole.
In the case where no stirrups are present along an edge, the reduced anchoring are
computed by multiplying the reinforcement degree by a reduction factor α, where
0 < α < α0. α0 represents the ratio of stirrups along the edge. If α0 = 1 full
utilisation of the rebar is assumed. The concept of the reduced strength due to
lack of suﬃcient anchoring is sketched in Figure 3.8. It is also possible to deﬁne an
anchoring length of the stringers.

3.1.2
Presentation of Results
Using the utilization solution provided by Nucleus, Nucleus returns with a factor
λ deﬁning the load level of failure. Additional to the failure load Nucleus provide
diﬀerent plots. All the plots provides the stress distribution for the speciﬁed load
combination and not the optimized solution found by the lower bound solution. This
also applies for the global reactions. If this is of interest the failure load found by
Nucleus should be added as a load combination in FEM-design. Only the plots rel-
evant for this report will be described below.
Nucleus provide the total shell forces in ULS instead of total stresses. Thus the forces
will be the same in the elements independent of the thickness, where as stresses is
diﬀerent depending of the thickness. The shell forces, is plotted as a colour palette
using blue and red colours (Figure 3.9), where red are positive forces and blue is
negative forces (tension is considered positive and compression negative). Both the
ULS and SLS calculation provides these plots for nx, ny, nxy, n1 and n2, where the
two latter is the principal forces. Nucleus also provides a plot showing the direction
of the principal stresses n1/n2 where blue is the direction of the compression forces
and red the tension forces, see Figure 3.10 .

Chapter 4
Veriﬁcation of Nucleus
In the following section test cases of simple known solutions from the literature are
presented and compared with results from Nucleus in order to verify the program.
The investigations performed in this section is just some preliminary investigations to
assess the limitations of the failure envelop implemented in the program presented
in Section 3.1. The investigation is preformed as the program will be used in an
actually design situation, i.e. using safety factors on the martial properties γs and
γc accounting for the uncertainties of the martial properties in order to design a
safe structure. The safety factors in respect to the material properties will of course
not be used in the compensation with experimental results. The details analytical
calculation for this Chapter are presented in Appendix A.

4.3
Concluding Remarks
It have now been establishes that in pure stress states and simple cases the failure
envelope (yield surface) implemented in Nucleus agrees well with the known solution
and analytical calculation. However it has been discovered that due to the simpliﬁed
yield surface presented in Section 3.1 Figure 3.4 Nucleus overestimate load-carrying
capacity when the failure is governed by the web crushing criteria.

Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:37==
Chapter 5
T-Beams
A test-series of simple supported T-beams subjected to combined shear and ﬂexure
done by Finn Bech et al. [8] are modeled and calculated in Nucleus. The results
determined by the lower bound solution given by Nucleus are then compared with
the results form the experiments.
The test set-up was a classical shear set-up with two symmetrically applied point
loads. The T-beams had diﬀerent cross-section dimensions and reinforcement ar-
rangements. The purpose of the experiments was to investigate the shear failure
mechanism and to investigate the dependence of the eﬀectiveness factor. All the
test use for this reports had vertical stirrups.
In this report the results from the experiments are used to compared the failure
mechanism and failure load with those ﬁnd by Nucleus. Furthermore the stirrup
stresses measured during the experiments are compared with the stresses calculated
by Nucleus. All the results will likewise be compared with analytical calculation
using the Theory of plasticity and the energy concept.
5.1
Reinforced concrete beams
In this section the theory of plasticity is used to describe the behaviour and failure
mechanism of the T-beams. Furthermore, the energy method will be outlined in
order to determine the stirrups stresses as a function of the external load.
Ultimate limit state
From the theory of the plasticity described in [1] the ultimate state for a simply
supported T-beams subjected to two symmetrical point-loads P can be derived. The
shear failure load for the T-beams with vertical stirrups is given by the web crossing
criterion as a lower bound solution. However, in order to assume that the theory of
the plasticity applies a minimum of horizontal and vertical reinforcement is required.
The requirements are based on that the reinforcement should be dominant compared
to the tension capacity of the concrete. If the reinforcement degree is low the capacity
may be controlled by a brittle fracture of concrete and not reinforcement, illustrated
on Figure 5.1 as the lower solid line.
23
==PAGE:38==
5.1.
Reinforced concrete beams
T-Beams
σ
ε
Figure 5.1. Eﬀect of concrete tensile strength.
In order to insure that the tensile strength of the concrete do not becomes dominant
a minimum reinforcement ratio is required.
Assuming that the reinforcement is
stressed to yield, the reinforcement ratio necessary to transfer ftef, is determined by
[1]
ρfY = ftef

The theory deﬁning the textitweb crossing criterion assumes that the compression
zone is idealized as an stringer carrying a compression force C and the tensile zone
(the concentrated reinforcement in the bottom of the cross-section) carrying a force
T. The concrete compression zone is positive in compression and the reinforced
tensile zone is positive in tension. In the cases of T-beams the web is considered
as the shear zone with a constant thickness b. As a simpliﬁcation the height of the
share zone z is chosen to be equal to the distance between the compression and
the tensile stringer. Studies done by Bach et. al [7] shows that it is a reasonable
assumption. The assumption as also been implemented in Eurocode[9]. The shear
span a is considered to have a constant shear force V . The load are transferred to
the shear zone by simplifying the boundary conditions assuming rigid beam ends
and transverse diaphragms near the action point of the load, see Figure 5.2. In this
24
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark

case the shear force V = P where the relation between the shear force and the shear
stress can is obtained as
τ = V
bz
(5.4)
A lower bound solution require a admissible stress ﬁeld. This is satisﬁed by assuming
a homogeneous stress ﬁeld in the web called the diagonal compression ﬁeld, sketched
in Figure 5.2 developed by Nielsen [14]. The uniaxial compression in the concrete
i named σc forming a constant angle θ with the x-axis, referring to the x, y-axis
deﬁned in Figure 5.2, also deﬁned as the second principle direction, σ2. The model
assumes that the web is cracked at failure and the cracks at failure will be formed
parallel to the direction of σ2.
Figure 5.2. Simply supported T-beam loaded by a two-point symmetrical loading with an illustration
of the assumed stress ﬁeld with diagonal compression ﬁelds in the web. The picture is borrowed
from [1].
The total stresses in the assumed stress ﬁeld is carries by a contribution from the
concrete and the stirrups depending on the direction. When the stirrups are some
what closely spaced the forces in the stirrups can be assumed to be smeared over
the concrete area as an equivalent stirrup force

Serviceability Limit State
As mentioned previously it is possible to achieve full utilization of both shear rein-
forcement and concrete when designing a reinforced concrete beams in the ultimate
state using plastic theory. However, under certain circumstances this may lead to
yield of the stirrups long before the failure load is reached. In some cases may yield
occur perhaps already under service load witch may lead to unacceptable width of
the cracks. It is therefore important to consider the elastic state.
This can be done by using the energy method. The theory is based on minimizing
the complimentary elastic energy which makes is possible to deduce compatible or
approximately compatible strain ﬁelds from an statical admissible stress ﬁeld. The
Theorem of the Minimum Complementary elastic energy is expressed as:
Of all stress ﬁelds satisfying the given boundary conditions the one which corre-
sponds to a compatible state of deformation makes the complementary elastic energy
a minimum.[2]
Below is outline way it is possible to use the complimentary elastic energy to analyse
the Serviceability Limit State.
When using the energy concept the potential energy is often considered, the problem
with this, is that it is given in the therms of displacement. However considering the
complimentary potential energy which is given in the therms of stresses, it is possi-
ble to determine the compatible value of tan θ, and from this determine the stirrups
stresses at cretin load level.
Considering an elastic element in respect to energy conservation the elastic energy
per unit volume can be expressed as [2]
28
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:43==
T-Beams
5.1. Reinforced concrete beams
W ′ =
Z
σxdεx +
Z
σydεy +
Z
τxydγxy
(5.21)
where σ and τ denote the normal- and shear stresses, respectively. Likewise ε and γ
denote the longitudinal- and shear strains, respectively. In the one-dimension case
the energy is represented as the area beneath the curve in a stress-strain diagram, as
illustrated in Figure 5.4. Introducing the concept of complimentary elastic energy
witch as the name indicted is the energy which is not a part W ′ but its complimentary
energy C′. The physical interpretation of the complimentary elastic energy C′ could
be expressed as the areal of the opposite part of the curve than W ′, as indicated in
Figure 5.4. For a two-dimensional case the complimentary elastic energy per unit
volume can be denoted as
C′ =
Z

Z

Z

(5.22)
W ′ and C′ are equal quantities for linear elastic materials.
Figure 5.4. Energy deﬁnitions in the one-dimensional case [2].
Idealizing that the reinforcement and concrete is linear elastic materials the potential
energy Πp and the complementary potential energy Πp may be expressed as
Πp =

where V and A indecates that the integral i done in respect to the volume and the
boundary, respectively. f represents the forces in the element, p the surface forces
and ∗indicates that the values are prescribed, for example, a beam subjected to a
given deformation. It is important to notice; if no deformation is prescribed, Πc is
equal to the complementary elastic energy which can be used to ﬁnd the compati-
bility equations.
The basic idea is to stablish a statical admissible stress ﬁeld by setting up the
equilibrium conditions and by considering the complimentary energy and thereby
determine a compatible strain ﬁeld. The advantages of applying the theorem of

minimum complementary elastic energy that it does not require a direct formula-
tion of the compatibility requirements, but that they may be obtained numerically.
Furthermore, in the case where the compatibility cannot be satisﬁed exactly, the
theorem will provide the best approximate solution meaning that if the stress ﬁeld
are chosen the solution will provide the parameters of the stress ﬁeld which best
satisﬁes the compatibility requirements.
The T-beam, illustrated in Figure 5.2 is now considered in the elastic state. The
inclination of the compressive stress in the web are now determined by minimiz-
ing the complementary potential energy by using the solution presented in [16].
Assuming that the concrete and reinforcement are linear elastic and the Poissons
ratio for both materials are zero, and no prescribed deformation, the complimen-
tary potential energy is equal to the complimentary elastic energy. By use of the
boundary condition and the stress distribution presented in Figure 5.2 it is only nec-
essary to consider the elastic energy of the shear span,C = Cs + Cc where Cs is the
contribution from the reinforcement and Cc is the contribution from the concrete.
Previously investigations of numerical calculations of θ [17] shows that the concrete
can be disregarded assuming that the contribution from the concrete is Cc << Cs
still getting a good approximation of θ. This means that only the contribution from
the reinforcement has to be considered, thus only the following expression has to be

5.2. Test description
A direct formulation in order to calculate the crack width, will not be treated in this
thesis but has been treated by Morten Bo Christiansen in [2].
Due to the parameters z and ν an experimental veriﬁcation of Equation (5.14) and
the calculation done by Nucleus will always be open for discussion. Nevertheless,
this will still be tried in this report by use of experience from previous work [1], [8]
and [7].
5.2
Test description
The T-beams had a total length of L = 3800mm with a shear span of a = 1050mm
and a total span of 3000mm. To achieve anchoring of the ﬂexural reinforcement the
supports are placed 400mm from the beam end. The load is applied with a focal
distance of 900mm. In order to archive shear failure of the beams it requires a large
ﬂexural capacity of the beams. The advantages of T-beams as test specimen in a
shear-test is that it is fairly simple to archive large bending capacity due to the top-
ﬂange of the T-beams acting as a large compression zone achieving a large internal
lever moment arm z. All the test specimens had a ﬂange width of 800mm. In order
to create a "test-zone" (the shear span) in the beams, additional stirrups are added
as, illustrated in Figure 5.5.
Figure 5.5. Elevation of test specimen. The variable shear reinforcement is not shown [8].
The T-beam test-series in [8] are divided in sub-series having a speciﬁc cross-section
layout for each sub-series. The shear reinforcement a varied within each sub-series.
At each experiment were determined the failure load and mechanism, the stresses
where measured in two stirrups at each shear span together with the stresses in the
concentrated reinforcement. The stresses where measured by strain-gauges located
after the principles indicated in Figure 5.6.
Figure 5.6. Posistion of strain-gauge [8].
In the test where noted a failure load for each shear span with a deviation up to
12%. In this report, the failure load is taken as the average between the two result.
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
31
==PAGE:46==
5.2.
Test description
T-Beams
Thus an unreliability of 6% may be expected when comparing the test-results with
numerical calculation.

5.3
Veriﬁcation of model
As mentioned previously the geometry of the specimen has to be modelled in Strusoft
FEM-design before importing the geometry in to Nucleus. As Nucleus only deals
with wall elements from FEM-design, two diﬀerent ways of modeling the T-beams
is plausible:
1. Two walls with diﬀerent thickness into the plane, on top of each other, see
Figure 5.11.
Figure 5.11. Model alternative 1.
2. Two thin high walls perpendicular to each other, see Figure 5.11.
34
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:49==
T-Beams
5.3. Veriﬁcation of model
Figure 5.12. Model alternative 2.
The advantages and disadvantages of the two models will be discussed below.
Model 1)
The beam is model in FEM-design as two wall element. The web is
modeled as a 200mm thick wall with a height of 310mm. The ﬂange of the T-beam
is model with a thickness of 800mm into the plane and a hight of 90mm. Nucleus
consider the two wall element as one structure without a construction joint between
the two elements, however the shear reinforcement must be speciﬁed for each wall
element corresponding to the individual beams in the sub-series.
The concern of this model is the ﬂange ability to transfer shear in the full width of
the ﬂange, which is not representative, and here by overestimate the bearing capacity.
The inﬂuence of the transfer of shear in the ﬂange are investigated by considering
diﬀerent version of the model and calculate the failure load in Nucleus. For the test
is use the reinforcement layout of Beam U6004.
First is tested the cross-section without any shear reinforcement compared with a
model of the cross-section also without shear reinforcement but where the width of
the ﬂange is equal to the width of the web. This is done in order to evaluate the
contribution of the concrete in the ﬂange to the shear capacity. The results are
presented in Figure 5.13 and 5.14.
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark

Figure 5.13 and 5.14 shows that the ﬂanges without any reinforcement contributes
with ca. 5 kN to the bearing capacity corresponding to ≃4%. Therefore, the con-
crete in the ﬂanges is in itself not contributing signiﬁcant to the shear capacity.
Now comparing the original cross-section with shear reinforcement with a model
where the shear reinforcement in the ﬂange is put to zero, one ﬁnds that the added
stirrups in the ﬂanges contributes with 51.5kN i.e. almost 20% to the shear capacity ,
see Figure 5.15 and 5.16. This indicates that the transfer of shear in the ﬂanges have
an signiﬁcant inﬂuence on the bearing capacity. It should be noted that in all four
cases the failure mode was of course shear failure. From the experiments, the failure
load of beam U6004 was found to be 227kN, which means that the reinforcement
layout in Figure 5.16 gives the results closest to the failure load from the experiment.
However, this reinforcement layout creates problems for the Nucleus model because
edge cursing of the concrete may occur in the program. This happens in the area
where there are no stirrups to distributes the stresses, causing the stresses in an
element (of the mesh) along the edge to exceed the concrete tension.
To reduce the eﬀect of shear transfer in the web and to account for the shortcomings
of the simpliﬁed yield surface discovered in Section 4.2.2 νn is put to νv, i.e. νn = νv.
The failure load for the the original cross-section are hereby reduced to 259.4 kN,
see Figure 5.17.

Model 2)
The beam is model in FEM-design as two thin heigh wall elements per-

thick wall with a height of 3800mm and a length of 310mm. The ﬂange of the T-beam

Nucleus consider the two wall element as one structure without a construction joint
between the two elements, however the shear reinforcement must be speciﬁed for
each wall element corresponding to the individual beams in the sub-series.
Creating a structural element in a ﬁnite element program as FEM-design, the pro-
gram creates an analytical model for each element. Also refereed to as projection
planes [18]. When structural element are put together as model 2, a analytical ali-
ment between the two element is created. The projection plane in the center of the
ﬂange are matched with the webs "vertical" plane, as illustrated in 5.18.

height because the model maybe only take half of the ﬂange in to account as the

overestimate the failure, although νn = νv. Thus, Model 2 is found to be the best
model to use in replicate the T-Beams. Its ﬁts the test-results best, and it has the
lowest amount of uncertainties that may aﬀect the results.

In the following section the test-results will be compared with the numerical results

be discussed followed by the Serviceability limit state. Lastly the overall behaviour of

5.4.2
Serviceability Limit State
In the design phase of structural elements the Serviceability Limit State (SLS) is
checked to incur that unacceptable crack width and deformation do not occur at
service load. For this purpose Nucleus should be able to determined the stresses in
the reinforcement in order to predict whether the stirrups had yielded under service
load causing unacceptable cracks widths.
Thus, in this section the stirrup stresses from the experiments will be compared
with the stresses found from a linear elastic calculation preformed by Nucleus. As
a reference the results will be compared with results determined by minimization of
the complementary elastic energy. However, ﬁrst is analysed the angle of the cracks
θ in order to understand the process the beams has been undertaken when exposed
50
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:65==
T-Beams
5.4.
Comparison of test results with numerical calculation
to external loading. The angle is determined from pictures taken during the test
and compared with the analytical and numerical calculation.
In this section each beam in each sub-series will not be evaluated individually but
results from certain beams representing the general behaviour will be presented and
discussed.
The results of U6025 will be left out due to clear ﬂexural failure in
the experiment. For further details in relation to the calculation for each beams is
refereed to Appendix B.
Analyse of θ
In order to understand how mush the stresses has to be redistributed in order to go
from the elastic state to the ultimate state, the angle of the cracks in the ultimate
state is also considered in this section. The cracks in the ultimate state forms the
angle θp in relation to the horizontal axis. The angle can be determined by Equation
(5.15). The angle in the fully caked state in the elastic range may for the T-beams be
determined by Equation (5.27) having minimized the complementary elastic energy.
θ is not directly given by Nucleus but can be calculated by considered the concrete
stresses σcx, σcy and τcxy in the shear span. Setting op the stresses in a diagonal

in to separate stresses in order to determined θpNC. Considering the total forces
at the middle of the shear span at the middle of the web, it is assumed that the
forces in the x-direction only is carried by the concrete, hence no horizontal web
reinforcement is present for the T-beam:

cracked. The angle of the second principle direction, ie, the angle θe of the cracks in

cracks are marked with blue and have an angle between θ = 28◦and θ = 32◦. Thus,
both the numerical- and analytical gives a rather good estimate of the angle of the
cracks in the linear elastic range comparing with the angle measured on the pictures.
Reaching failure the angle of the principle directions has to move from θe to θp in
order to go from the elastic range to the plastic range. This require a rather ductile
specimen. From analytical calculation using Equation (5.15) θp = 14◦and from the
numerical calculation θ is found to θpNC = 13◦. Looking at the new cracks at failure
P/Pmax = 1, marked with red, the angle determined by the pictures are found to be
between θ = 13.6◦and θ = 21◦. Again proving that the numerical- and analytical
calculation of θ in the ultimate state gives a rather good estimate of θ, and the
beams ability to redistribute the stresses. All the angle determined by Nucleus and
the energy method for each beam are listed in Appendix ??.

At low reinforcement degrees the stresses in the concrete has to be undertaken a large
redistribution in order to go from the elastic range to the plastic range. Increasing
the amount of stirrups the required redistribution of the stresses in the concrete
becomes smaller which lead to smaller crack width.
Stirrup stresses
In this section the stresses measured in the stirrups during the experiments are plot-
ted as a function of the load. From the numerical calculation in Nucleus, the stresses
in the stirrups may be determined by considering the angle tan θeNC or by consid-
ering σwy at diﬀerent load levels. The results from Nucleus and the experiments
are compared with the stresses determined by minimizing the complimentary elastic
energy using equation (5.28).
In order to analyse the development of stresses in the stirrups, is it important to
understand the inﬂuence of the location of the strain-gauge measuring the stresses
in respect to the developments of the creaks. Again, the results from U6004 are used
to establish some general therms in order to describe and analyse the process the
beams has been undertaken when exposed to external loading.
The strain-gauge measuring the stirrups stresses is located as illustrated in Figure
5.32 numbered from 1 to 4. The stirrups with strain-gauge will be named in accor-
dance with the number of the strain-gage attached to it.
The results for the stirrup stresses are presented in Figure 5.34 together with the
results from numerical- and analytical calculations.

Pmax.

Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark

5.4.
Comparison of test results with numerical calculation
Figure 5.34. Stirrup stresses as a function of the load for U6004.
The largest stresses occurs in stirrup 4 which consists with Figure 5.32 showing that
a crack is formed where bstrain-gauge 4 is located. As the load increases so do the
stresses in the concrete until the tension capacity of the concrete is reaches, indicated
by point (a) for stirrup 4. After this point initial cracks occurs and concrete soften-
ing occurs until a certain crack width is reached, indicated by point (b). From this
point the concrete no longer contributes only the reinforcement. From this point the
curve of the stresses in the stirrups is parallel to the solutions from the numerical-
and analytical calculations. For stirrup 1 and 2 point (a) is ﬁrst reached at a load
level of approximately 0.4. Considering the pictures in Figure 5.32 no cracks are
present at the location of the strain-gauge, thus the stirrups are ﬁrst activated much
later than stirrup 4.
In Figure 5.34 the load level Pt0.5 and Pfm0.5 are plotted as vertical lines correspond-
ing to τ = 0.5fct and τ = 0.5√0.1fc, respectively. fct is the tensile strength of the
concrete determined by test in [8] listed in Table 5.1. No direct link between point
(a) and the presumably reduced tensile strength is found for beam U6004. Due to the
fact that it is diﬃcult to determine the tensile strength of concrete by test and the
unknown presence of micro cracking, a large margin of error of the tensile strength
may be expected. However, the best estimate for U6004 is found by determine the
tensile strength from the concrete compression strength and not the tensile test.
The changes in the stresses marked with the area (c) is due to the shift of the second
principle direction (θe −θp) from the linear elastic range to the ultimate state.

5.4.
Comparison of test results with numerical calculation
From Figure 5.47 it is seen that three (marked on the ﬁgure) results do not follows
the path formed by the other results. The results marked on the Figure are also
marked with red in Table 5.48. Deviation were also found for the three beams in
the ultimate state (Figure 5.26 and 5.31). U6013c where found to be governed by
1
2νfc in Nucleus instead of the lower bound solution stated in (5.14) and failure
due to ﬂexure were found for both U6026c and U4213m in Nucleus, but not in the
tests. Disregarded these three beams, there appears to be a relation between the
reinforcement ratio and the horizontal translation of the stresses due to the tensile
capacity of the concrete. Hence, at small degrees of vertical reinforcement the tension
capacity of the concrete has a large eﬀect on the result in the elastic range compared
to a large degree of shear reinforcement. However, it seems that at ψ/ν > 0.5 the
inﬂuence of the concrete stagnates. The inﬂuence of the concrete strength in respect
to the parallel translation in the elastic range consists with the tendency found by
experiments of tension specimens by Falker and Rostasy, see Figure 5.49
Figure 5.49. Stress-stain curve from experiment by Falker and Rostasy [19].
Thus, at low reinforcement degrees the stresses will be governing by the tensile
strength of the concrete witch complies with the tendency discovered in Figure 5.33.
Nucleus manages to calculate the stresses in the stirrups, however it predicts that
yielding occurs before it acutely does, compared to the stresses found by the exper-
iments. In fact, even at low reinforcement ratio yield do not occur at service load
taken as 0.5 −0.6Pmax. This is due to the contribution from tensile strength of
the concrete creating a horizontal translation of the curve with the factor K. Even
though the elastic range is deﬁned by reversible change of the material the theory
dose not jet covers the inﬂuence of micro cracking of the concrete under load- un-
loading of the material in the elastic range. If the beams was loaded until 0.4Pmax
and then unloaded and loaded again, K might had change to a smaller value due to
a reduction of the concrete stiﬀness. Hence, it is on the safe side to assume K = 0
predicting yielding of the stirrups sooner than it actively does.

Chapter 6
Deep Beam
In this section a test-series of simple supported deep beams done by Leonhardt and
Walther [20] will be calculated in Nucleus and analysed in respect to the results
from the experiments. The deep beams will only be analysed in the ultimate limit
state due to limitation in Nucleus in respect to the numerical model of the beams,
using rigid materials. However, the distribution of principal forces given by Nucleus
will be compared in respect to the crack patten in order to establish rather Nucleus
provides a realistic stress distribution in the beam under loading, and perhaps be
able to predicts the direction of the cracks.
The test series includes in total 9 specimen with diﬀerent reinforcement layout and
diﬀerent cross-section. But conmen for them all, they have a combination of concen-
trated reinforcement and mesh reinforcement in both directions of the web. Some of
the beams had a special reinforcement layout with heavily bend concentrated rein-
forcement counting 4 of the beams. These will not be used as test specimens in this
report, because it is not possible to modelled the bend reinforcement as this report
was written. However, one of the beams with the bend reinforcement will be use as
an reference in order understand some of the failure modes. The test-series were di-
vided in to two groups: Group 1. were all uniformly loaded at the Top face. Group
2. were all uniformly loaded at the bottom face (y=150mm). The two diﬀerent
set-up is shown in Figure 6.1 and 6.2, respectively.
Figure 6.1. Test set-up Grope 1 [20]
Figure 6.2. Test set-up Grope 2 [20]

==PAGE:82==
6.1.
Load-carrying capacity
Deep Beam
The uniformly load was applied in a extent of 0.8L and the width of the support is
0.1L for both cases.
In this Chapter a general description of the experiments, theory, reinforcement,
material properties and Nucleus model will ﬁrst be presented, after which every
specimen will be treated and analysed individually.
6.1
Load-carrying capacity
A deep beam is deﬁned as a structural element where the span is lees than three
times the height of the cross-section [9]. The deep beams in the test-series have all
the ratio L
h = 1 < 3, where h is the height of the cross-section and L the span.
Often deep beams have a height so the margin of design do not require shear re-
inforcement. However, in order to control the crack width shear reinforcement is
necessary. In accordance to M. P. Nielsen [3] should be placed reinforcement (ver-
tical and horizontal) corresponding to a reinforcement ratio ρ of 0.2%. If the rein-
forcement ratio are diﬀerent in the two directions the crack width may be controlled
by insuring that the requirements stated in appendix F [9] are met; the minimum
requirement is fulﬁlled if 1
2ρ′
x ≤ρx ≤2ρ′
x and 1
2ρ′
y ≤ρy ≤2ρ′
y, ensuring that the re-
distribution of stresses in the concrete is diminished, reducing the evolving of cracks.
The bearing capacity of deep beams with a combination of homogeneous rein-
forcement and concentrated reinforcement may be determined from a standardised
method combining grid analogy and consideration of homogeneous stress ﬁelds. The
standardised method requires a low horizontal reinforcement ratio. For the homoge-
neous reinforcement considering stress ﬁeld the solution is found to be equal to the
solution obtained by considering the yield moment, as long as L/h is of a certain size.
The relation is illustrated in Figure 6.3 showing the carrying capacity for Φ = 0.1
in witch the moment in the center cross-section is M = 1
2pbL2[1]. The solution for
the concentrated reinforcement is also governed by ﬂexural capacity.

6.1. Load-carrying capacity
Figure 6.3. Exact solution for a rectangular disk supported along the lateral faces.
Thus, if the ratio of the horizontal reinforcement is low the load-carrying capacity is
govern by the ﬂexural capacity. From test an practical experience it seems reason-
able to assume that if the total reinforcement degree in a vertical section is less than
about 0.4/ fc(fc in MPa), bending failure will govern the load-carrying capacity [1].
Because, if the amount of concentrated reinforcement is relatively small the principle
illustrated in Figure 6.3 will partly be valid. This means that the failure mechanism
may be seen either as shear or ﬂexural, thus they are governed by approximately the
same load level when the mesh reinforcement is isotropic.

Reinforcement Layout
Deep Beam
There are noticed some inconsistencies in the declaration of the concentrated rein-
forcement for beam WT7 in the experimental article [20]. In text it is stated that
the reinforcement consists of 8φ 8mm reinforcement. However on all the drawings
and pictures of the reinforcement is only shown 6φ 8mm. In these report 6φ 8mm
is obtained as the concentrated reinforcement for WT7 as illustrated in Figure 6.8.
The requirement stated in Equation (6.1) is found to be fulﬁl for all beams, hence
the capacity described in Section 6.1 applies, see Appendix C. For Group 2 the
vertical reinforcement are found without problems able to transfer the failure load
to the top face.
Furthermore it should be noticed the requirements of minimums reinforcement is
not satisﬁed in the directions where φ5/260 is applied thus ρ = 0.15% < 0.2%.
Nevertheless, the method in Section 6.1 and the beams will still be used to compare
with the results provided by Nucleus.
In order to insure full anchoring length of the concentrated reinforcement (bend-
ing reinforcement) the main reinforcement of the beams WT2, WT3, WT4, WT6
and WT7 was anchored with horizontal placed "hooks" bended somewhat narrow
(radius= 4mm) and the "hooks" are wrapped in pairs by wires (φ2mm) in a spiral,
see Figure 6.9. By means of these "hooks" which is special unfavourable in the case
of thin structural elements, the pressure will be stored in the bend of the narrow
"hooks" causing failure in the concrete. However, in this way (by use of wires in
a spiral) the compression zone inside the "hooks" is slightly reinforced. The mesh
reinforcement is closed with U-bars at all free edges.
Figure 6.9. Concept for anchoring of the concentrated reinforcement, [20].
The reinforcement layout of specimen WT1 is also shown, Figure 6.10. WT1 is not
modelled in Nucleus and the results not compared as mentioned previously. However
the reinforcement layout is used for further investigating of the failure mode.

6.4
Nucleus model
The test specimens had diﬀerent cross-section design, thus the geometrical design
of the beams in FEM-Design has to be modelled individually. The model for each
beam will be presented under the individual section. Her will only be outlined some
general principles for the models.
All the deep beams are models as a 1600mm high and 1600mm long wall element.
Some of the beams are split in to several elements in order to simulate the devia-
tions in some of the beams geometry. In order to simulate fully anchoring of the
main reinforcement a small cube is modelled at the bottom of each side of the main
beam, where the stringers (representing the main reinforcement) are extend to. The
material properties for the small cubes are chosen to have the name rigid, see Figure
6.11. From this, Nucleus know that this area should be considered rigid, creating
full anchoring of the bars crossing the area, as mentioned previously in Section 3.1.1.
Rigid materials are not jet included in the SLS calculation of Nucleus, thus an com-
pression in the serviceability state (elastic range) has not been done for this series.
The reinforcement mesh is set to have 100% U-bars at all free edges. Delaunay is
chosen as the mesh type due to variations in the cross-section.
The material parameters for the concrete used in the model are listed in Table 6.1.
If the load-carrying capacity is found to be governed by failure at the support νn is
put to 1 other wise νn = νm in Nucleus. For the reinforcement is used fu in Table
6.2 and Es = 210GPa.

6.7
Deep Beam WT4
Specimen WT4 is equipped with an increased cross section at the supports with a to-
tal thickness of 200mm, a width of 160mm. The increase cross-section has a constant
thickness in a hight from 0mm-150mm. Hereafter, the thickness of the cross-section
is degrease to 100mm from y=150mm-750mm, see Figure 6.6. The increased cross-
section is refereed to as a pilaster. The rest of the specimen has a constant thickness
of 100mm. In order to model the increased cross-section the beam is separated in
to diﬀerent wall elements as illustrated in Figure 6.25. It was not possible to model
an element with diﬀerent thickness in top and bottom of the element. An attempt
to model the upper part of the pilaster with the average thickness of 150mm have
been tried. However, this creates a collapse mode governed by a failure between the
area of the pilaster with the thickness of 200mm and the area with the thickness of
150mm. Thus the upper area of the pilaster is set to b=200.
A mesh size of 0.05 is chosen for the entire model in order to create enough element
between the boundaries of the diﬀerent wall elements, see Figure 6.26.

Deep Beam WT6
Specimen WT6 is a part of Group 2, i.e. load applied at the bottom face of the

width of 400mm. The load is applied on both sides of the enlarged cross-section as
illustrated in Figure 6.7. Stirrups are added in the enlarged cross-section in order
to transfer the load from the bottom to the top face of the beam.
The beams is modeled as two wall elements: 1) a wall elements with the dimension
h=1450mm, L=1600mm and b=100mm and 2) a wall elements with the dimensions
h=150mm, L=1600mm and b=400mm. Element 1) and 2) has added two reinforce-
ment mesh one with φ10/69 vertical and zero horizontal and one with zero vertical
and φ5/260 horizontal. This is due to diﬀerent strength of the reinforcement witch
is not possible to deﬁned for one mesh in Nucleus, therefore 2 reinforcement mesh.
The concentrated reinforcement is added as speciﬁed in Figure6.7. The model is
shown in 6.30. The load is applied y=150mm in the bottom face of the model. The
mesh size is chosen to 0,03 where the result is found to stagnate.

The failure mechanism is in the experimental report described as failure of the in-

occur in the middle of the beam perpendicular to the span. Moving outwards the
88
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:103==
Deep Beam
6.9. Deep Beam WT7
direction of the compression stresses forms a lightly arch form from the support to
the center in the top of the beam with perpendicular tension stresses. Predicting
the crack pattern from Figure 6.35 the cracks will perhaps be form horizontal in
the middle of the beam perpendicular to principal direction. Mowing outwards the
cracks will rotate following the direction of the principal compression stresses, still
perpendicular to the direction of the principal tension stresses witch will force the
concrete to crack. This is exactly what had occurred looking at the crack pattern
in Figure 6.34, slightly arched cracks with an angle comparable with the principal
direction found in Figure 6.35 and horizontal cracks in the center line of the beam,
created by tension perpendicular to the span.

from Nucleus of WT6.

distribution present in the beam under loading quite well, despite the fact that the
minimum requirement of reinforcement is not fulﬁlled in one direction and the re-
inforcement degree in the y-diﬀraction are more than 2ρx. Even though the elastic
cracked state not is analysed this gives rather good indication of that numerical solu-
tion gives a good replication of the structures ductility, i.e. it ability to redistribute
the stresses.

The failure mechanism is due to failure of the concrete just above the enlarges cross-

load found by the analytical and numerical calculation are not quite comparable. It
is diﬃcult to exactly pinpoint what this failure mechanism may arise from. It might
be due to the compression strut forming in the concrete which narrows near the sup-
port crating a large compression in the section where the width of the cross-section
changes from 100mm to 400mm or just due to poorly executed reinforcement or
concrete. If it is the ﬁrst case Nucleus is found to overestimate the bearing capacity
being to optimistic in distributing the stresses. Is it the later case nothing can be
concluded from comparing the failure load.

rection of principal tension stresses. Again indicating that the stress distribution

6.10
Concluding remarks
For deep beams with a combination of concentrated reinforcement and evenly dis-
tributed reinforcement in the web in both direction it is diﬃcult to determine the
failure load and mechanism. Some simpliﬁed method has been developed but limit
to some speciﬁc requirements. Thus, using a computed program as Nucleus is prefer-
ably for these issues. Comparing the results from Nucleus with test results done by
[20] are obtained a relatively good similarity of the results. However, in cases were
the failure load found by the experiments to exceeds the concrete strength at the
support, Nucleus under estimates the load-carrying capacity signiﬁcantly due to the
limitation of the program. However, comparing with practical design of structural
elements using analytical calculation Nucleus provides a solution corresponding to
the analytical results. Over all Nucleus is found to provide a better estimate of the
load-carrying capacity than the Analytical solution provides, either the failure load
is governed by ﬂexural failure or failure at the support. For Group 1. the results
from Nucleus for WT2 and WT3 (failure at support) underestimates the capacity
with 27.8% where as the analytical calculation deviates 28.8%. These lathes devia-
tion between the results might be due to trixatik stress state occur at the support
cause by the main reinforcement. WT4 was also governed by failure at the sup-
port. The deviation was found to 1% and hand calculations 1.2%. Comparing the
results for Group 2 Nucleus deviates 10.3% and the analytical calculations deviates
92
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:107==
Deep Beam
6.10. Concluding remarks
22.4%. Is WT7 disregard do to failure of concrete in the web near the support the
deviation is found to 4.2% against 14.8% for the analytical calculation.
Further
more, the standardised analytical lower bound solution where surprisingly found
to overestimate the load-carrying capacity for WT6 and WT7, where as it should
have provided a safe solution and at lest lower than the optimized solution found
by Nucleus. The deference between the experiments and Nucleus in respect to the
load-carrying capacity are summarized in Table 6.10.

A compression of the SLS has not been don to do limitation of the program in the

to be useful in order to predict the crack patron of the specimen and further more
determine rather the numerical solution provides a good prediction of the structures
ductility. It was found hat the cracks occurred perpendicular to the direction of
the principal tension stresses and follows the shape of the principle direction in
compression.
Further more it can be concluded that Nucleus provides a rather
good estimation of the stress distribution in the beam under loading and pereicts
the beams ability to redistribute the stresses even though the requirements to the
reinforcement are not fulﬁlled for the beams.

Chapter 7
Continuous Deep Beams with Web
Openings
In the design phase of structural elements as wall and deep beams, openings from
electrical pipes an wires gives rise to many problems, because openings has not yet
been covered by most codes of practice or standardised calculation methods. For
this a program like Nucleus providing a numerical optimized lower bound solution
is really favourable to use in respect to the cost of material and calculation times.
In this Chapter test of "reinforced concrete continuous deep beams with web open-
ings" in the interior shear span done by Ashour and Rishi [24] will be modelled and
calculated by Nucleus and the results compared. The continuous deep beam being
a statical undetermined structure. The comparison will only be carried out for the
Ultimate Limit State due to limitation of the program in respect to the numerical
model, which also was the case in Chapter 6. All the input data for all the tables in
this section may be found in Appendix D.
7.1
Test Description
In total 6 beams with openings in two diﬀerent sizes (small and large) are inves-
tigated, divided in to 3 groups in respect to the web reinforcement, each group
includes two beams; one with a big opening and one with a small opening. For
each group are provide a reference beams without web openings done by the ﬁrst
other Ashour [25]. All the beams have the same outer geometry, a overall high of
h = 625mm, a width of 120mm, a total length of Ltotal = 3000mm and a span of
L = 1340mm, with a hight-span relation of L
h = 2.1 < 3, thus the beams are deﬁned
as Deep Beams according to Eurocode [9], as mentioned in Chapter 6. A elevation
of the test specimen are illustrated in Figure 7.1 and the dimension of the holes are
illustrated in Table 7.1. All the beams have the same main reinforcement.
95

7.2.1
Reinforcement layout
The 3 Groups are refereed to as Group A, B and D. Group A has a large amount
of vertical and horizontal reinforcement, Group B has a low amount of vertical and
horizontal reinforcement and Group D have only vertical web reinforcement. The
original test series also included a group C with non vertical reinforcement. This
group are excluded from the investigating done in this report, thus the numerical
calculation are based on reinforcement in both directions requiring a minimum re-

The reference beams without web openings are named CDB1, CDB2 and CDB4
referring to group A, B and D, respectively. The solid beams have exactly the same
reinforcement as the group they refer to, just without the openings. No additional
reinforcement are placed around the openings. The rebars crossing a opening are
just cut to ﬁt the opening. The reinforcement layout for the solid beams representing
the three Groups A, B and D are presented in Figure 7.3, 7.3 and 7.3, respectively.

7.3.1
Specimens with Web Openings
The test specimens had the same geometry and main reinforcement, the only vari-
ation is the web opening and web reinforcement. Thus, 3 diﬀerent basic model is
generated in FEM-deign before exporting is to Nucleus. The beam i modelled as a
120mm thick wall element with a total length of 3000mm and a hight of 625mm. In
order to simulate fully anchoring of the main φ12 reinforcement bars a small cube is
modelled at the top and bottom of each side of the main beam element, where the
98
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:113==
Continuous Deep Beams with Web Openings
7.3. Nucleus Model
stringers (representing the main reinforcement) are extend to. The material prop-
erties for the small cubes are chosen to have the name rigid, see Figure 7.6. Thus,
Nucleus know that this area should be considered rigid, creating full anchoring of
the bars crossing the area, as mentioned previously in Section 3.1.1. Still the rigid
materials are not jet included in the SLS calculation of Nucleus, thus an comparison
in the Serviceability Limit State has not been done for this series. The main rein-
forcement bars with the diameter φ10 is modelled in the center of the beams with
the length in each span illustrated in Figure 7.1.
Figure 7.6. Numerical model of Continuous Deep Beam with small openings.
In Nucleus is create a model for each beam using the basic model from FEM deign
containing the geometry and main reinforcement. The material parameters for the
concrete used in the model are listed in Table 7.3. The properties use for the rein-
forcement is listed in Table 7.2 and Es = 210GPa. The horizontal web reinforcement
is modelled as one mesh without stirrups, thus the model doe not assume stirrups
at the openings horizontally. However, the vertical web reinforcement has to be
creates as diﬀerent mesh as illustrated in 7.7 to avoid stirrups above and beneath
the openings and still have closed stirrups at the other free edges.
Figure 7.7. Numerical model of Continuous Deep Beam with large openings with illustration of web
reinforcement.

mesh type is Delaunay and the results are found to stagnate at a mesh size of 0.03,
see Figure 7.8.

7.3.2
Reference Test CDB
The reference test CDB1, CDB2 and CDB4, are modelled in the same manner as

Chapter 8
Conclusion
Dealing with the design of reinforced concrete structure the ultimate load capacity is
often of interest. This can be determined by the theory of plasticity which assumes
full utilization of both concrete and reinforcement. This creates rather slender ele-
ments with low reinforcement degrees which may cause problems in respect to large
cracked width and early failure.
In this report a comparison between numerical models used in a programme de-
veloped by Rambøll called Nucleus, and experiments from the literature have been
carried out in respect to the Ultimate limit state. The stress ﬁeld in the elastic
cranked state has also been investigated in order to asses the degree of stress redis-
tribution.
Through the report it was discovered, that the results deviated signiﬁcantly in re-
spect to which model approaches was used.
Especially in the case of statically
undetermined structure, it was found, that it is not at all safe to relay on the load
distribution proved by the program. However, if the structure is forced to use the
load distribution following best practice, the program provides a rather god estimate
of specimens behaviour.
8.0.1
Ultimate limit state
By use of the optimized lower bound solution implemented in Nucleus, the use of the
program was in general found to provide a good estimate of the failure load and the
behaviour in the ultimate limit state. Thus, a good compliance between the failure
envelope (material model) implemented in Nucleus and the experiments was found.
Some issues, however where found when the failure load was governed by the web
crushing criterion, due to a simpliﬁed yield surface implemented in the programme
to minimize the calculation time. However the problem is found to be very limited
to specimens where the specimen is over reinforced en one demarcation (Specimen
U6013c and U4213m marked with red in Table 8.1).
Even though Nucleus provides a lower bound solution, the load-carrying capacity,
disregarding the specimens governed by the web crushing criterion, where found in
some cases to be larger than the failure load found by experiments. However, the
deviation found between these results where found to be within acceptable range,
when comparing with test-results. Thus, in respect to eﬀective strength of the con-
crete the eﬀectiveness factors νv = 0.8 −
fc
200 and νm = 0.98 −
fc
500 where found to
provide a good estimate of the reduced strength do to micro-cracking.
107
==PAGE:122==
Conclusion
Conclusion
In almost all cases, the solution provided by Nucleus where found to be a better
estimate of the load-carring capacity, than the solution provided by analytical cal-
culation. In the cases where the analytical calculation where governed by cussing
of the concrete at the support, Nucleus complied with the analytical calculation
(marked with green in Table 8.1) and not the signiﬁcantly higher failure load found
by the experiment. It is suggested that the large diﬀerence between the results is
due to triaxial stress state according to the test-specimen which Nucleus due not
account for, thus the yield surface is bases on a biaxial stress state.
When the load-carrying capacity where found to be governed by 1
2νfc the analytical
solution provided a signiﬁcant lower failure load than provided by Nucleus. This was
due to heavily concentrated reinforcement creating a small internal lever arm, thus
the analytical solution assuming that the shear hight is equal to the internal lever
moment arm. By use of Nucleus the actual shear height could be found, providing
almost an exact solution compared to test-results.
In Table 8.1 a summary of all results from the diﬀerent specimens investigated
throughout this report are presented. The results highlighted with red is either due
to early failure caused by of poorly executed specimen(WT7) or due the simpliﬁed
yield surface implemented in Nucleus, overestimating the capacity.
